    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="879cb92dfc154683b92da1348b69a50b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '0361b8a0-7857-4ed4-9e99-e68d723901e7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d130560783" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Organic bipolar electrodes can undergo both n-type and p-type reactions for energy storage due to numerous active sites, which can usually deliver a high theoretical specific capacity. Herein, a new bipolar polymer cathode material PQPZ is designed and synthesized. The electron-donating phenazine group undergoes a two-electron redox reaction which can store anions, the electron-withdrawing phenanthraquinone can store sodium-ions through a two-electron redox reaction. Notably, as a bipolar cathode material for sodium-ion batteries, PQPZ exhibits a reversible specific capacity of up to 270 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 1.0–4.0 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and achieves a high energy density of 696 Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> in a half-cell. A capacity retention rate of 90% is obtained after 300 cycles at a current density of 0.5C. More excitingly, even after being cycled for 10000 cycles at 10C, the PQPZ electrode shows an average decay rate as low as 0.0036% per cycle, manifesting a very stable cycling performance. In addition, PQPZ also shows excellent performances when tested at −10 °C, making it promising in practical applications. Considering the bipolar character of PQPZ, symmetric batteries are also successfully constructed, fulfilling the “ready-to-charge” property without a pre-activation process. The results reveal the potential application of PQPZ in practical use.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411362-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411362-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Currently, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely used in energy storage technology.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0001_R_d130560775e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0003_R_d130560775e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The further development of high-energy density lithium-ion batteries is limited by the reserves of transition metal (Co/Ni/Mn) on earth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0004_R_d130560775e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0005_R_d130560775e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is urgent to develop sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with higher energy density and lower costs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0006_R_d130560775e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Energy">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0007_R_d130560775e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Current Opinion in Green and Sustainable Chemistry">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At present, inorganic cathode materials have been extensively explored in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0008_R_d130560775e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Storage Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0010_R_d130560775e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Energy Chem.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the inorganic materials usually are expensive and toxic, more severely, their volume will have significant changes during charging and discharging, resulting in low cycling performance and poor reversibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0011_R_d130560775e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Sci.">11</a>-<a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0013_R_d130560775e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Organic electrode materials (OEMs) have abundant reserves and are eco-friendly, furthermore, their properties can be easily regulated through structural modifications, which has the potential to achieve high gravimetric energy density through rational molecular design.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0014_R_d130560775e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Chem. A">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0015_R_d130560775e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Commun.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, they are insensitive to the size of metal ions and can accommodate sodium ions with larger radii than lithium ions, which is beneficial for the rapid insertion/removal of sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0016_R_d130560775e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0017_R_d130560775e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> OEM can be divided into n-type and p-type based on the different internal transport carriers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0018_R_d130560775e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a>-<a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0020_R_d130560775e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ChemSusChem">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The n-type materials receive external electrons at the LUMO level under the electric field, so they can store cations. The n-type materials typically have high specific capacity, while low operating voltage limits their application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0021_R_d130560775e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angewandte Chemie-International Edition">21</a>-<a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0023_R_d130560775e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Acs Applied Polymer Materials">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The p-type materials can achieve a high working voltage and are usually reported as battery cathodes. However, the high working voltage may cause other side reactions, leading to irreversible capacity and low coulombic efficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0024_R_d130560775e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For bipolar materials, the existence of n-type moiety could adjust the energy level of the material, resulting in moderate working voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0025_R_d130560775e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although introducing electron-withdrawing groups could tune the energy level, this will also increase the molecular weight and may lead to a decrease in specific capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0026_R_d130560775e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the rational design of electron-withdrawing groups with redox activity could increase the working voltage while maintaining a high specific capacity. For dual ion batteries, although anions and sodium ions are embedded/detached on the anode and cathode during the charging/discharging process, causing changes in electrolyte ion concentration, the anions will return to the electrolyte during the discharging process, resulting in the regeneration of the electrolyte. As a matter of fact, dual-ion batteries can work stably.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0027_R_d130560775e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Energy Lett.">27</a>, <a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0028_R_d130560775e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, due to the flexible and adjustable structure of organic materials, bipolar materials can be designed to work as both the cathode and anode for organic symmetric sodium-ion batteries. This design does not require any pre-activation, which can reduce material costs and simplify the battery manufacturing process.</p>
         
         <p>In this article, we designed and synthesized a new D-A type polymer (PQPZ) as the cathode for sodium-ion batteries. PQPZ is composed of the electron-withdrawing 9,10-phenanthraquinone and the electron-donating dihydrophenazine groups. The phenazine unit is p-type that can store anions in the electrolyte and undergoes a two-electron redox reaction. The 9,10-phenanthraquinone is a sodium storage active site, thereby maintaining a high capacity. The electron-withdrawing nature contributes to a lowered HOMO energy level in PQPZ, which increased the working voltage. The polymerization effectively inhibits material dissolution. In half cells, the PQPZ electrode shows reversible performances, with a high discharge specific capacity of 270&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the voltage range of 1.0–4.0V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), quite close to its theoretical specific capacity of 277 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The result reveals that the active sites in PQPZ could be well utilized. In particular, the cathode showed an average capacity decay rate of only 0.0036% per cycle after 10000 cycles. In addition, PQPZ was subjected to cycling tests at 60 °C and −10 °C, revealing excellent performances. Notably, at −10 °C, even after 100 cycles at 10C, the discharge capacity remained as high as 104 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating a remarkable capacity retention rate of 98%. Moreover, considering the bipolar feature of PQPZ, we prepared a symmetrical battery, where PQPZ acts as both the cathode and anode materials. A reversible discharge capacity of 119 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 100 mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> was observed, with a high energy density of 116 Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> and stable operation in 200 cycles. These results indicate that PQPZ has significant practical applications in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411362-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411362-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Polymerization has long been known as an efficient means to inhibit the dissolution of organic compounds in electrolytes. The Buchwald-Hartwig reaction between 5,10-dihydrophenazine (donor) and the BPQ affords the terminal polymer PQPZ (<b>Scheme</b> <a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0011">1</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0029_R_d130560775e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 9,10-Phenanthraquinone (PQ) and diphenylphenazine (DPZ), were used as reference materials and are discussed in the following content. PQ was directly obtained from a commercial supplier, while DPZ could be facilely synthesized following the literature method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0030_R_d130560775e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Org. Chem.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electron-rich dihydrophenazine and the electron deficient 9,10-phenanthraquinone provide excellent charge transfer properties for the newly synthesized D-A copolymer. The electron deficient phenanthraquinone could tune the energy level, meanwhile to store sodium ions, and thus the electronic performances of the electrode could be improved. The solubility of 2,7-Dibromophenanthrene-9,10-dione (BPQ), DPZ, and PQPZ in electrolyte solvents was tested. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (supporting Information), after soaking the three materials in electrolyte for seven days, the solvent with BPQ and DPZ showed significant color changes, while that with PQPZ did not show any significant color changes, indicating that the polymerization could significantly reduce their solubility in the electrolyte.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411362-fig-0011"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/13cc8c1d-2e8f-4a2a-96a0-f8afc5618fa3/adfm202411362-fig-0011-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/13cc8c1d-2e8f-4a2a-96a0-f8afc5618fa3/adfm202411362-fig-0011-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cfefd770-f6c6-4291-ae5f-b795dbf71a34/adfm202411362-fig-0011-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/13cc8c1d-2e8f-4a2a-96a0-f8afc5618fa3/adfm202411362-fig-0011-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411362-fig-0011&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411362" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Synthetic routes for PQPZ. b) The structures of PQ and DPZ.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) is used to characterize the molecular structure of PQPZ. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0001">1a</a> shows the Fourier transform infrared spectra of BPQ, PQPZ, and DPZ. There are two strong typical peaks in BPQ, located at 1672&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1590&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> respectively, belonging to the stretching vibrations of C═O and C═C. The peaks between 900 and 1300&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> could be attributed to aromatic ring vibration, the peak at 833&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to out of plane C─H, and the peak at 709&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to C-Br.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0031_R_d130560775e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ChemSusChem">31</a>-<a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0033_R_d130560775e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It can be seen that there are still two strong typical peaks of C═O and C═C at 1672&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1590&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in PQPZ, indicating the presence of PQ units in PQPZ. Compared with the reactants, the C-Br bond in PQPZ basically disappeared, and a new peak appeared at 1272&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the C─N characteristic peak, as observed in DPZ, indicating the presence of PZ units in PQPZ.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0034_R_d130560775e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Eng. J.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results confirm that the target polymer PQPZ was obtained as expected.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411362-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d34e5587-01c5-4eb8-bece-3d4a067d8eb3/adfm202411362-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d34e5587-01c5-4eb8-bece-3d4a067d8eb3/adfm202411362-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a127a84e-31f5-48b8-a068-82e23943d278/adfm202411362-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d34e5587-01c5-4eb8-bece-3d4a067d8eb3/adfm202411362-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411362-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411362" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) FT-IR of the BPQ, PQPZ and DPZ. b) EDS analysis of PQPZ.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The EDS results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0001">1b</a> indicate that the content of Br element in the target polymer PQPZ is very low, aligning with the FT-IR data shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0001">1a</a>. Furthermore, elemental analysis was carried out to determine the elemental composition of PQPZ. The experiment results suggested element contents of C 78.663%, H 3.86%, O 7.72%, and N 6.34% in PQPZ, which agrees well with the calculated values shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (supporting Information). The result also confirms the successful synthesis of the material. FE-SEM of the PQPZ powder (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, supporting Information) revealed bulk aggregates. Powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) was also utilized to determine the crystallinity of PQPZ. PQPZ exhibits an amorphous morphology. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a> (supporting Information) shows two broad diffraction peaks at 15° and 19°, which may be attributed to the π-π stacking in PQPZ.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0035_R_d130560775e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411362-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411362-sec-0030-title">2.1 Theoretical Calculations</h3>
            
            <p>Density functional theory (DFT) was used to examine the frontier molecular orbital distributions and energy levels. Periodic boundary condition calculations were performed on PQPZ. In addition, two reference materials, PQ and DPZ were also studied for comparison.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0036_R_d130560775e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Angewandte Chemie-International Edition">36</a>, <a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0037_R_d130560775e638" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Phys. Chem. A">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here the active sites of n-type cathodes are located on the Lowest Unoccupied Molecular Orbital (LUMO), while the active sites of p-type materials are distributed on the Highest Occupied Molecular Orbital (HOMO). The optimized molecular structures and orbital distributions are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0002">2</a>. Note that the HOMO of PQ is mainly located on the n-orbital of the O in the carbonyl group and the LUMO is predominantly located on the carbonyl groups, suggesting their role as active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage. While the HOMO of DPZ is mainly on the phenazine unit, its LUMO orbital is widely distributed throughout the molecule, indicating the potential of phenazine to store PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>. For the PQPZ, LUMO is distributed on the phenanthraquinone unit, while HOMO is concentrated on the phenazine unit. The completely separated HOMO and LUMO orbitals manifested the bipolar feature in PQPZ, which facilitates the storage of both sodium ions and anions. Specifically, the introduction of the electron-deficient 9,10-phenanthraquinone resulted in a decreased HOMO energy level in PQPZ (<i>E</i><sub>HOMO</sub> = −4.46&nbsp;eV) compared to DPZ (<i>E</i><sub>HOMO</sub> = −4.31&nbsp;eV). Consequently, the p-type redox potential in PQPZ is enhanced relative to DPZ, because the HOMO level is negatively correlated with the potential of the p-type reaction. Furthermore, a lower LUMO energy level in PQPZ (<i>E</i><sub>LUMO</sub> = −3.55&nbsp;eV) than PQ, suggesting a higher n-type redox potential. The results should be the consequences of the extended conjugation during polymerization which narrows the bandgap in PQPZ polymer.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411362-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2214a4e9-c58c-44b4-93f3-9aac8db1ee03/adfm202411362-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2214a4e9-c58c-44b4-93f3-9aac8db1ee03/adfm202411362-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/85967234-a93d-4cde-85a2-26f74b7d4576/adfm202411362-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2214a4e9-c58c-44b4-93f3-9aac8db1ee03/adfm202411362-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411362-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411362" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Chemical structures and the optimized molecular orbital distributions of PQ and DPZ, as well as molecular orbital distributions calculated by periodic boundary condition of PQPZ.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411362-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411362-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Behaviors and Half-Cell Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical behavior of PQPZ was initially assessed by cyclic voltammetry in sodium-ion half cells, and details of the coin cell fabrication can be found in the Supporting Information. The electrolyte used was 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) in bis-(2-methoxyethyl) ether (DIGLYME). We tried some common carbonate-based electrolytes in our experiments, such as 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC: DEC = 1:1&nbsp;Vol%, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC: DEC = 1:1&nbsp;Vol%, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC: DEC = 1:1&nbsp;Vol% with 5% FEC. However, PQPZ half cells showed poor performance in these electrolytes, exhibiting low capacity. Therefore, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DIGLYME was used in our work. As illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0003">3a</a>, four distinct reversible redox peaks were observed in the potential range of 1.0–4.0V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) in the first cycle. The first and second redox peaks, located at 1.6/2.1 and 2.4/2.6V, respectively, correspond to the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and deintercalation processes. The third and fourth redox peaks at 2.93/3.06 and 3.72/3.82&nbsp;V, belong to the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> intercalation and deintercalation processes. Both the PQ unit and PZ unit exhibit a two-electron redox reaction, which is also a two-step process. Moreover, the CV cycle curves of the 2nd to 4th cycles overlap well, indicating that PQPZ has good redox stability and reversibility. We further analyzed the dQ/dV curves of PQPZ (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0003">3b</a>), showing a matched voltage plateau with the CV curve. The discharge-charge curves of PQPZ-based half cells in the first, second, fifth, and 10th cycles are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0003">3c</a>. The half cells are activated in the first discharge cycle, and four discharge-potential platforms are observed, which are 3.7, 2.9, 2.4, and 1.6V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), which are consistent with the CV curves. Notably, PQPZ can realize an initial discharge capacity of 270&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and a high energy density of 696&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, with a medium working potential of 2.85V. These results suggest a 4-electron redox mechanism for each repeating unit in PQPZ. In contrast, DPZ exhibits two pairs of redox peaks at 2.95/2.5 and 3.7/3.5V, while PQ exhibits two pairs of redox peaks at 1.55/1,77 and 1.85/2.11V, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0003">3d</a>). Compared to DPZ and PQ, the oxidation-reduction potential of PQPZ is elevated, which is consistent with the above DFT calculations.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411362-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f41f0b69-3b99-439f-ab8d-15e97995ef58/adfm202411362-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f41f0b69-3b99-439f-ab8d-15e97995ef58/adfm202411362-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5487323d-2a97-4ab4-8eaf-fad4d8b60049/adfm202411362-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f41f0b69-3b99-439f-ab8d-15e97995ef58/adfm202411362-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411362-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411362" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical characterization of PQPZ: a) CV curves at 2&nbsp;mV/s; b) dQ/dV curves at 0.5C. c) Charge–discharge curves at 0.5C; d) CV curves of DPZ, PQPZ, and PQ.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The cycling performance of PQ, DPZ, and PQPZ at 0.5C is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0004">4a</a>. The PQ cathode exhibits an initial specific capacity of 108&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. However, PQ is more easily soluble in the electrolyte, resulting in a significant decrease in specific capacity in subsequent cycles. Compared to PQ, DPZ has a larger molecular structure and exhibits relatively better cycling performance. After the battery was activated, the reference DPZ electrode showed an initial capacity of 159&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, while after 190 cycles, only 67.9% of the original value was retained. Excitingly, the PQPZ electrode exhibited a discharge-specific capacity of 270&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycle, which is close to the theoretical value. The high electrochemical activity of PQPZ comparable to the theoretical capacity implied efficient utilization of almost all active sites. Even after 300 cycles, PQPZ retained a significant capacity of 243&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, representing a high retention rate of 90%. Subsequently, the rate performance at different current densities was measured and shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0004">4b</a>. The discharge capacities of PQPZ at 0.5/1/2/3/5/8/10C were 252/216/191/179/165/152/146&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (1C = 277 mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Upon decreasing the current density to 0.5C, the recorded discharge-specific capacity was 220&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0004">4c</a>, PQPZ exhibited an initial discharge capacity of 145&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10C. More importantly, a discharge capacity of 96&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> could still be maintained even after 10,000 cycles, with the average capacity decay rate as low as 0.0036% per cycle. Especially, the scanning electron microscope (SEM) image in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (supporting Information) shows that the initial electrode morphology is basically consistent with that within 100 cycles. After 1000 cycles, some small particles were observed from SEM images, which may be caused by the oxidation/reduction or doping/dedoping of PQPZ. There are no significant morphological changes in the electrode even after 10000 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0038_R_d130560775e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ChemSusChem">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This also indicates that PQPZ has excellent cycling stability. This performance surpassed numerous organic cathode materials reported to date, as indicated in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (supporting Information). In addition, we calculated the “charge average” discharge (V<sub>discharge</sub>), charge (V<sub>charge</sub>), and net-average voltage of PQPZ according to the method reported in the literature as the true average voltage, and represented it in the form of changes in the number of cycles in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0005">5</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0039_R_d130560775e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Chem. A">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The variation of the “charge average” voltage is very small, even during the entire duration of the constant current cycle (viz., 300 cycles), which is consistent with the cycling stability of PQPZ. The excellent cycling stability of PQPZ strongly validates the rational design of PQPZ. PQPZ is not sensitive to air or moisture, it can be stored in the air for two months without any changes, making it potential for practical applications.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411362-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e52f85cd-78c6-4c70-98fb-be412ef38a02/adfm202411362-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e52f85cd-78c6-4c70-98fb-be412ef38a02/adfm202411362-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c79bf1dc-7c31-469b-96fa-5a71fca42945/adfm202411362-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e52f85cd-78c6-4c70-98fb-be412ef38a02/adfm202411362-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411362-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411362" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycling performance at 0.5&nbsp;C of PQPZ, DPZ and PQ. b) Rate performance at different current densities of PQPZ. c) Long-term cyclability at 10&nbsp;C of PQPZ.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411362-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/644ec45c-b662-4f8f-a702-1c2473986ac1/adfm202411362-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/644ec45c-b662-4f8f-a702-1c2473986ac1/adfm202411362-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f0e34e0e-f320-4cfd-9a0a-ed1b228a3333/adfm202411362-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/644ec45c-b662-4f8f-a702-1c2473986ac1/adfm202411362-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411362-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411362" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Variations of ‘charge-averaged’ discharge (V<sub>discharge</sub>), charge (V<sub>charge</sub>) and net-average voltages with cycle number for a) PQPZ with b) corresponding zoomed-in view of first 20 cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To evaluate the potential applications of PQPZ in real conditions, the cycling performance of PQPZ was evaluated in sodium-based half cells at both high (60 °C) and low (−10 °C) temperatures. The discharge capacity of PQPZ after 100 cycles at 60 and 0.5C was found to be 244&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with a high capacity retention rate of 94%, as depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0006">6a,b</a>. After 600 cycles at 10C, the discharge capacity remained at a high level of 100&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Whereas, considering the fact that the electrolyte NaPF<sub>6</sub> tends to precipitate when the temperature is less than or equal to −20 °C, the cycling performance of PQPZ was tested at −10 °C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0040_R_d130560775e881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0041_R_d130560775e881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At −10 °C, PQPZ maintained discharge capacities of 155 and 104&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.5 and 10C, respectively, with high capacity retention rates of 85% and 98%, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0006">6c,d</a>. These results demonstrate that PQPZ can maintain excellent cycling performance across a wide range of temperatures, indicating promising commercial applications.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411362-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/39af0b09-df6e-433a-85fb-7e4c28b74644/adfm202411362-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/39af0b09-df6e-433a-85fb-7e4c28b74644/adfm202411362-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/34d56309-6cdd-42be-a657-1283f3a0563e/adfm202411362-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/39af0b09-df6e-433a-85fb-7e4c28b74644/adfm202411362-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411362-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411362" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Cyclic performance at 60&nbsp;°C at a) 0.5C and b) 10C. Cyclic performance at −10 °C at c) 0.5C and d) 10C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>A series of systematic tests were conducted to assess the electrochemical kinetics of PQPZ, to investigate the factors that contribute to the excellent performances. In order to study charge transfer kinetics, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was tested at different cycles. An equivalent circuit was used to fit the EIS data (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, supporting Information). Initially, the charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) of PQPZ was measured to be 196.5&nbsp;Ω, which notably decreased to 123.9&nbsp;Ω after the first cycle. The decrease in R<sub>ct</sub> may be due to effective wetting between the electrode and electrolyte, which leads to good contact and larger interface formation, thereby improving charge transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0042_R_d130560775e921" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">42</a>, <a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0043_R_d130560775e921" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Chin. J. Chem.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CEI has a particular function in maintaining electrolyte and cathode stability, and the interface impedance is very stable from the first cycle to the 50th cycle (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, supporting Information), indicating that polarization is very small during cycling, and the SEM image of the electrode after cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, supporting Information) still maintains its original state, indicating that CEI is stable and robust during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0044_R_d130560775e933" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Energy Chem.">44</a>, <a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0045_R_d130560775e933" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Energy Storage Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, we believe that the presence of CEI will reduce the impedance of the battery, which is consistent with the stable cycling performance of PQPZ.</p>
            
            <p>The CV curves obtained at scanning rates ranging from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> is displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0007">7a</a>. Analysis of the graph reveals a noticeable trend, that was, with an increase in scanning rate, the anode peak shifts to a higher potential, and the cathode peak shifts to a lower potential. This shift occurs as a consequence of the elevated scanning rate that induces cathodic polarization. The linear relationship between peak current and scanning rate is fitted, and the b-value determined by the slope of the fitted line could reflect the reaction kinetics, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0007">7b</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0046_R_d130560775e952" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Angewandte Chemie-International Edition">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, for peaks A1–A4, the b-values for PQPZ are determined to be 0.68, 0.90, 0.86, and 0.81, and for peaks C1–C4, they are 0.75, 0.85, 0.96, and 0.85, respectively. Notably, the b value nearing 1.0 in the PQPZ cathode signifies behavior akin to that of a capacitor, revealing that the PQPZ electrode had fast reaction dynamics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0047_R_d130560775e958" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411362-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/33bb70b9-25cc-4765-8b04-e31e68805464/adfm202411362-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/33bb70b9-25cc-4765-8b04-e31e68805464/adfm202411362-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f4f43b6b-0d3f-47a9-adb6-d29dc039ba33/adfm202411362-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/33bb70b9-25cc-4765-8b04-e31e68805464/adfm202411362-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411362-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411362" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The CV curves of PQPZ at different scan rates from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Fitting lines of the b-values for PQPZ. c) The CV profile with capacitive and diffusion behaviors of PQPZ at a scan rate of 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. d) Capacitive contributions at different scan rates. e) GITT curves. f) The calculated Na-ion diffusion coefficients.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">To assess the impact of diffusion and capacitance on cathode capacity, the current is separated into two components: diffusion (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub> <i>v</i> <sup>1/2</sup>) and capacitance (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub> <i>v</i>), as shown in the equation below:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202411362-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8be6469f-6322-47fa-a16a-bd19684df230/adfm202411362-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202411362:adfm202411362-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202411362-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>i</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = {{k}_1}\ v + {{k}_2}{{v}^{1/2}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The electrochemical behavior of the PQPZ cathode is confirmed to be dominated by capacitance, as evidenced by the results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0007">7d</a>, where the capacitive contribution increases from 61% at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> to 82% at a scan rate of 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. This demonstrates the impact of scan rate on capacitive behavior. Additionally, the conductivity of ions plays a crucial role in the rate performance. To further investigate the ion diffusion coefficients (D) of PQPZ, we employed the Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique (GITT) using the following formula as a reference point:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202411362-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/ae4e4c2c-df7c-45ed-8035-d534cfce98c1/adfm202411362-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202411362:adfm202411362-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202411362-math-0002.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mn>4</mn>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>π</mi>
                                    <mi>τ</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfrac>
                              <msup>
                                 <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>m</mi>
                                             <mi>B</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>V</mi>
                                             <mi>M</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>M</mi>
                                             <mi>B</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                          <mi>S</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>
                                             <mi>s</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>
                                             <mi>t</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}D = \frac{4}{{\pi \tau }}{{\left( {\frac{{{{m}_B}{{V}_M}}}{{{{M}_B}S}}} \right)}^2}{{\left( {\frac{{\Delta {{E}_s}}}{{\Delta {{E}_t}}}} \right)}^2}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>In the above equation, the ∆E<sub>s</sub> stands for the difference in voltage between the initial and steady states of each step, and ΔE<sub>t</sub> is the total voltage change during a pulse. Both ∆E<sub>s</sub> and ΔE<sub>t</sub> are crucial factors in evaluating battery performance. In addition, the parameters τ, m<i><sub>B</sub></i>, <i>V<sub>M</sub></i>, S, and M<i><sub>B</sub></i> represent pulse time, mass loading, molar volume, electrode interface area, and molar mass, respectively. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0007">7f</a>, the ion diffusion coefficient exhibits a noticeable decline when the cell is charged above 3.5V and discharged below 1.5V. The decrease in ion diffusion coefficient might be caused by the repulsion of ions' charges during the embedding process. It is worth noting that compared with reported values, the calculated average ions diffusion coefficient for PQPZ in half cells is relatively high at 1.16 × 10<sup>−8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0048_R_d130560775e1119" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem. Eng. J.">48</a>, <a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0049_R_d130560775e1119" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Compos. Commun.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in PQPZ at different potentials were measured (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, supporting Information), and the average diffusion coefficients of D(Na<sup>+</sup>) and D(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>) were calculated to be 1.12 × 10<sup>−8</sup> and 6.26 × 10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. This value is higher compared to previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0050_R_d130560775e1147" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Chem. Commun.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high diffusion coefficients indicate the fast charge capability of the corresponding batteries. The results strongly verify that the rational molecular design of the PQPZ structure could significantly enhance the rate performance of batteries.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411362-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411362-sec-0050-title">2.3 Energy Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the redox mechanism of the PQPZ cathode, FTIR was measured at different voltage states, and the results are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0008">8</a>. The absorption peak of C-N appears at 1272 cm<sup>−1</sup>. When charging from the pristine state to 4.00V, the absorption peak intensity of C─N gradually decreases and nearly vanishes at 4.00V. Meanwhile, a characteristic peak at 836 cm<sup>−1</sup> emerges and steadily increases, corresponding to the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, indicating PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> storage on phenazine during this stage. Upon discharge to 2.85&nbsp;V, the C─N and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> peaks revert to their original positions, suggesting reversible doping/de-doping of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> between 2.85 and 4.00&nbsp;V in PQPZ. The phenazine unit functions as the active site throughout the charging/discharging process. As the discharge process continues, the C═ peak at 1701&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> gradually diminishes, nearly vanishing at 1.00&nbsp;V. Then, when charged to 2.85V, C═O reappears. These results indicate that within the range of 1.00–2.85V, C═O groups on phenanthraquinone work as active sites for storing Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411362-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/de3ff1f7-154d-4e21-8eff-097ced148f38/adfm202411362-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/de3ff1f7-154d-4e21-8eff-097ced148f38/adfm202411362-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/55720588-1abc-4949-897a-fd0c0053151f/adfm202411362-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/de3ff1f7-154d-4e21-8eff-097ced148f38/adfm202411362-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411362-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411362" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Reaction mechanism of PQPZ. b) Charge–discharge curves and Ex Situ FTIR of PQPZ at different voltage states.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To clarify the energy storage mechanism of PQPZ, Ex Situ XPS was utilized. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0009">9</a> illustrates the F 1s and O 1s spectra of PQPZ at various potentials. In the initial F1s spectrum, the C─F peak at 688.48&nbsp;eV is associated with the presence of polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) as a binder. In the charging process, a P─F peak emerges, indicating that the phenazine unit stores PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, while the presence of Na-F peak should be related to the Na-F bond formed at the cathode electrolyte interface (CEI) layer. Na-F serves as a stable inorganic component in CEI, boosting the durability of cathode cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411362-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411362-bib-0051_R_d130560775e1218" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Mater. Chem. A">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the discharge continues, the intensity of the P─F peak decreases, indicating that the phenazine unit reversibly stores PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>. In the initial O 1s spectrum, only the C═O peak is observed. Upon discharging to 1.00&nbsp;V, the C─O peak (532.98&nbsp;eV) significantly emerges. After charging to 2.85&nbsp;V, the intensity of the C═O peak returns to its initial position. These results indicate that PQPZ can reversibly store Na<sup>+</sup> at 1.00–2.85V and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> at 2.85–4.00V, which is consistent with the FT-IR results mentioned above. In addition, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (supporting Information) employed density functional theory (DFT) to calculate the structural changes of PQPZ monomers during charge and discharge processes. It also illustrates the spatial distribution of boundary orbitals of PQPZ polymer monomers and the position of inserted counter ions. In order to quantify the sodium content, we conducted EDS testing on the PQPZ electrode discharged to 1V, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (supporting Information). It shows that the elemental mass fractions of Na and N are 5.49 wt.% and 3.37 wt.%, respectively. Therefore, the ratio of Na and N substances can be obtained as 0.991, which is very close to 1. This indicates that the amount of N in the phenazine unit is very close to the amount of Na<sup>+</sup> stored in the phenanthrenequinone unit, thus confirming the storage mechanism of PQPZ.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411362-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/240db58c-4419-439f-8afa-2ddd6c086d96/adfm202411362-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/240db58c-4419-439f-8afa-2ddd6c086d96/adfm202411362-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/962c7df8-fcdf-4867-a08f-1262f335b98e/adfm202411362-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/240db58c-4419-439f-8afa-2ddd6c086d96/adfm202411362-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411362-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411362" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">F 1s and O 1s spectra of Ex situ XPS of PQPZ at different voltage states.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411362-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411362-sec-0060-title">2.4 Organic Symmetric-Cell</h3>
            
            <p>Considering the bipolar characteristics of PQPZ. The voltage gap between 9,10-phenanthraquinone and phenazine units is 3.00V. The symmetric battery was assembled using PQPZ as the cathode and anode material. It is worth noting that these symmetrical batteries exhibit an interesting characteristic called “ready-to-charge”. Note that the electrolyte used in symmetric cells is the same as that used for half cells (1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DIGLYME). The cell configuration of the PQPZ symmetric battery is shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0010">10a</a>: The 9,10-phenanthraquinone unit in PQPZ acts as the negative electrode, while the phenazine unit acts as the positive electrode. The CV curve of a symmetrical cell with a scanning speed of 2mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> is depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0010">10b</a>. Its operating voltage ranges from 0.01–3.00&nbsp;V, with two redox peaks located at 0.65/0.70V and 1.68/2.00V, respectively. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0010">10c</a>, according to the total quality of PQPZ on the cathode and anode, a reversible discharge capacity of 119 at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;<sup>−1</sup>g is calculated. The value is close to the theoretical specific capacity of 138&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, as calculated from a two-electron reaction. Moreover, it shows a capacity retention rate of 57% and an energy density of 124.2&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. Notably, in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411362-fig-0010">10d</a>, even at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, a reversible discharge capacity of 92&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> can be provided, with a capacity retention rate of 78.2% after 300 cycles. The successful preparation of symmetrical batteries demonstrates the application prospects of PQPZ electrodes.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411362-fig-0010"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bfa7f380-9806-4e63-bc6b-555795aefdc7/adfm202411362-fig-0010-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bfa7f380-9806-4e63-bc6b-555795aefdc7/adfm202411362-fig-0010-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/847b5f9c-6ff5-4006-b762-a46565715139/adfm202411362-fig-0010-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bfa7f380-9806-4e63-bc6b-555795aefdc7/adfm202411362-fig-0010-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 10<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411362-fig-0010&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411362" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Illustration of symmetric organic full-cells. b) CV curves. Cycle performances of coin cells at c)100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and d) 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g−<sup>1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411362-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411362-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, we developed a new D-A type bipolar organic polymer (PQPZ) as cathode material for SIBs. The solubility of the cathode material in the electrolyte was significantly reduced by the polymerization. PQPZ exhibited a high energy density of 696&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> and a satisfactory specific capacity of 270&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in half cells at 1.0–4.0V. It is noteworthy that even after 10000 cycles, a high retention capacity of 96&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is obtained, with an average decay rate as low as 0.0036% per cycle. In addition, PQPZ can maintain excellent cycling performance at 60 and −10 °C. In a symmetrical-cell, PQPZ can still provide a reversible capacity of 119&nbsp;mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and an energy density of 124.2&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Our research indicates that organic polymer electrodes show exceptional cycling performance and material longevity in sodium-ion batteries, with the capability to consistently cycle over 10,000 times. This proves their promising prospects for commercial applications. Considering that electrolytes will be consumed during charging, there are still some challenges that need to be overcome, which may deserve more attention in future research works on sodium dual-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
